<p>The <b>Rwandan Civil War</b> was a conflict in the African republic of <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rwandan_Armed_Forces" title="Rwandan Armed Forces">Rwandan Armed Forces</a> (FAR), representing the government of President <a href="/wiki/Juv%C3%A9nal_Habyarimana" title="Juvénal Habyarimana">Juvénal Habyarimana</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_Patriotic_Front" title="Rwandan Patriotic Front">Rwandan Patriotic Front</a> (RPF), a rebel group composed mostly of refugees who had been resident in <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>. The war was fought in two phases. The first phase began on 1 October 1990, with an RPF invasion of the north east of the country, ending on 4 August 1993, with the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Arusha_Accords" title="Arusha Accords">Arusha Accords</a>. The second phase began following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Habyarimana" title="Assassination of Habyarimana">assassination of Habyarimana</a> and the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_genocide" title="Rwandan genocide">Rwandan genocide</a>. The RPF resumed fighting on 7 April 1994, and the civil war ended with an RPF victory on 18 July 1994.</p>
<p>The war had its origins in the long-running dispute between the <a href="/wiki/Hutu" title="Hutu">Hutu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tutsi" title="Tutsi">Tutsi</a> groups within the Rwandan population. The rift began in the ancient <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Rwanda" title="Kingdom of Rwanda">Kingdom of Rwanda</a>, with Tutsi kings enacting increasingly anti-Hutu policies through the nineteenth century, and grew wider during the <a href="/wiki/German_colonial_empire" title="German colonial empire">German</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belgian_colonial_empire" title="Belgian colonial empire">Belgium</a> colonial periods; both European nations ruled through the kings and perpetuated a pro-Tutsi policy. The Hutu population <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_Revolution" title="Rwandan Revolution">revolted</a> in 1959, supported by a Belgian administration which had reversed its allegiances. They massacred numerous Tutsi and ultimately established an independent, Hutu-dominated state in 1962; the revolution forced more than 100,000 Tutsi to seek refuge in neighbouring countries. The refugees lived in exile for several decades, agitating for a return to Rwanda, but they were not capable of mounting a serious attack. This situation changed in the late 1980s when <a href="/wiki/Fred_Rwigyema" title="Fred Rwigyema">Fred Rwigyema</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Kagame" title="Paul Kagame">Paul Kagame</a>, Rwandan Tutsi officers in the Ugandan army, took over leadership of the RPF and organised it into a battle ready military force.</p>
<p>The October 1990 invasion started well; benefiting from the element of surprise, RPF forces penetrated 60 km (37 mi) into Rwanda. They suffered a reversal of fortune on the second day, when leader Fred Rwigyema was killed in action. <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zaire" title="Zaire">Zaire</a>, strong allies of President Habyarimana, sent forces to Rwanda to assist the FAR; with their assistance, and benefiting from the loss of RPF morale after Rwigyema's death, the FAR gradually they regained all the ground the RPF had taken. The rebels were eventually pushed back to the Ugandan border on 30 October. Paul Kagame was in the United States at the time of the invasion, but when Kagame learned of Rwigyema's death, he departed immediately for Rwanda, to take command of the RPF troops. He withdrew most of the army to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virunga_mountains" title="Virunga mountains">Virunga mountains</a>, a harsh but secure location close to Ugandan border, where they could regroup, rearm, fundraise and recruit. After three months of inactivity, Kagame launched a surprise attack on <a href="/wiki/Ruhengeri" title="Ruhengeri">Ruhengeri</a>, taking the city in a single morning, before withdrawing into the mountains again. The RPF then began a classic hit-and-run style guerrilla war against the FAR, which continued for many months with neither side able to gain the upper hand.</p>
<p>However, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Habyarimana_and_Ntaryamira" title="Assassination of Habyarimana and Ntaryamira">assassination of Habyarimana</a> in April 1994 proved to be the catalyst for the <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_genocide" title="Rwandan genocide">Rwandan genocide</a>, the commonly quoted death toll for which is 800,000. The closely interrelated causes of the war and genocide led some observers to assume that the reports of mass killings were in fact some new flaring of the war, rather than a different phase. The RPF restarted its offensive, eventually taking control of the country. The <a href="/wiki/Hutu" title="Hutu">Hutu</a> government-in-exile then proceeded to use <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_refugee_crisis" title="Great Lakes refugee crisis">refugee camps</a> in neighboring countries to destabilize the new RPF government. The RPF and its proxy rebel forces prosecuted the <a href="/wiki/First_Congo_War" title="First Congo War">First Congo War</a> (1996–1997), which led in turn to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Congo_War" title="Second Congo War">Second Congo War</a> (1998–2003), all of which involved a Hutu force with the objective of regaining control of Rwanda. Thus while the civil war officially lasted until 1993, some literature has the war ending with the RPF capture of Kigali in 1994 or with the disbanding of the <a href="/wiki/Refugee" title="Refugee">refugee</a> camps in 1996, while some consider the presence of small rebel groups along the Rwandan border to mean that the civil war is ongoing.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rwandan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-independence_Rwanda_and_origins_of_Hutu.2C_Tutsi_and_Twa">Pre-independence Rwanda and origins of Hutu, Tutsi and Twa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rwandan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-independence Rwanda and origins of Hutu, Tutsi and Twa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rwanda_Nyanza_Mwami_Palace.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of King's palace in Nyanza, Rwanda depicting main entrance, front and conical roof" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Rwanda_Nyanza_Mwami_Palace.jpg/220px-Rwanda_Nyanza_Mwami_Palace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Rwanda_Nyanza_Mwami_Palace.jpg/330px-Rwanda_Nyanza_Mwami_Palace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Rwanda_Nyanza_Mwami_Palace.jpg/440px-Rwanda_Nyanza_Mwami_Palace.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rwanda_Nyanza_Mwami_Palace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A reconstruction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_Rwanda" title="King of Rwanda">King of Rwanda</a>'s palace at <a href="/wiki/Nyanza,_Rwanda" title="Nyanza, Rwanda">Nyanza</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_Hutu,_Tutsi_and_Twa" title="Origins of Hutu, Tutsi and Twa">Origins of Hutu, Tutsi and Twa</a></div>
<p>The earliest inhabitants of what is now <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a> were the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Twa" title="Great Lakes Twa">Twa</a>, a group of aboriginal <a href="/wiki/Pygmy_peoples" title="Pygmy peoples">pygmy</a> hunter-gatherers who settled in the area between 8000 BC and 3000 BC and remain in Rwanda today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien200344_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien200344-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMamdani200261_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMamdani200261-3">[3]</a></sup> Between 700 BC and 1500 AD, a number of <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a> groups migrated into Rwanda, and began to clear forest land for agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien200358_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien200358-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMamdani200261_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMamdani200261-3">[3]</a></sup> The forest-dwelling Twa lost much of their habitat and moved to the slopes of mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing200775_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing200775-5">[5]</a></sup> Historians have several theories regarding the nature of the Bantu migrations; one theory is that the first settlers were <a href="/wiki/Hutu" title="Hutu">Hutu</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Tutsi" title="Tutsi">Tutsi</a> migrated later and formed a distinct racial group, possibly of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cushitic" title="Cushitic">Cushitic</a> origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199916_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199916-6">[6]</a></sup> An alternative theory is that the migration was slow and steady, with incoming groups integrating into rather than conquering the existing society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMamdani200258_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMamdani200258-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMamdani200261_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMamdani200261-3">[3]</a></sup> Under this theory, the Hutu and Tutsi distinction arose later and was a class distinction rather than a racial one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien200369_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien200369-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShyaka10.E2.80.9311_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShyaka10.E2.80.9311-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The population coalesced, first into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clans_of_Rwanda" title="Clans of Rwanda">clans</a> (<i>ubwoko</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien200388.E2.80.9389_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien200388.E2.80.9389-10">[10]</a></sup> and then, by 1700, into around eight kingdoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien2003482_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien2003482-11">[11]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Rwanda" title="Kingdom of Rwanda">Kingdom of Rwanda</a>, ruled by the Tutsi Nyiginya clan, became the dominant kingdom from the mid-eighteenth century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien2003160_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien2003160-12">[12]</a></sup> expanding through a process of conquest and assimilation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorsey199438_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorsey199438-13">[13]</a></sup> and achieving its greatest extent under the reign of King <a href="/wiki/Kigeli_IV_of_Rwanda" title="Kigeli IV of Rwanda">Kigeli Rwabugiri</a> in 1853–95. Rwabugiri expanded the kingdom west and north,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMamdani200269_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMamdani200269-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien2003160_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien2003160-12">[12]</a></sup> and initiated administrative reforms which caused a rift to grow between the Hutu and Tutsi populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMamdani200269_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMamdani200269-14">[14]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berlin_Conference_(1884)" title="Berlin Conference (1884)">Berlin Conference</a> of 1884 assigned the territory to <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Germany</a>, which began a policy of ruling through the Rwandan monarchy, and supporting Tutsi chiefs around the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199925_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199925-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Belgian_colonial_empire" title="Belgian colonial empire">Belgian forces</a> took control of Rwanda and <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199925.E2.80.9326_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199925.E2.80.9326-16">[16]</a></sup> and from 1926 began a policy of more direct colonial rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199926_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199926-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien2003260_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChr.C3.A9tien2003260-18">[18]</a></sup> The Belgians modernised the Rwandan economy, but Tutsi supremacy remained, leaving the Hutu disenfranchised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199935_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199935-19">[19]</a></sup> In 1935, Belgium introduced identity cards labelling each individual as either Tutsi, Hutu, Twa or Naturalised. While it had previously been possible for particularly wealthy Hutu to become honorary Tutsi, the identity cards prevented any further movement between the classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGourevitch200056.E2.80.9357_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGourevitch200056.E2.80.9357-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolution.2C_exile_of_Tutsi.2C_and_the_Hutu_republic">Revolution, exile of Tutsi, and the Hutu republic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rwandan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Revolution, exile of Tutsi, and the Hutu republic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_Revolution" title="Rwandan Revolution">Rwandan Revolution</a></div>
<p>After 1945, a Hutu counter-elite developed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002108_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002108-21">[21]</a></sup> leading to a deterioration in relations between the groups; the Tutsi leadership agitated for speedy independence to cement their hold on power,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199943_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199943-22">[22]</a></sup> while the Hutu elite called for the transfer of power from Tutsi to Hutu,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199945.E2.80.9346_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199945.E2.80.9346-23">[23]</a></sup> a stance increasingly supported by the church and the colonial government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002113_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002113-24">[24]</a></sup> In November 1959, the Hutu began a series of riots and arson attacks on Tutsi homes, following false rumours of the death of a Hutu sub-chief by Tutsi activists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarney2013124_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarney2013124-25">[25]</a></sup> Violence quickly spread across the whole country, beginning the <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_Revolution" title="Rwandan Revolution">Rwandan Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarney2013125_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarney2013125-26">[26]</a></sup> The king and Tutsi politicians attempted a fightback,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewbury1988196_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewbury1988196-27">[27]</a></sup> seeking to seize power and ostracise the Hutu and Belgians,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewbury1988195_196_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewbury1988195_196-28">[28]</a></sup> but were thwarted by Belgian colonel <a href="/wiki/Guy_Logiest" title="Guy Logiest">Guy Logiest</a>, who was brought in by the colonial governor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewbury1988196_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewbury1988196-27">[27]</a></sup> Logiest re-established law and order, and began a programme of overt promotion and protection of the Hutu elite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarney2013127_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarney2013127-29">[29]</a></sup> The Belgians then replaced many Tutsi chiefs and sub-chiefs with Hutu, and consigned the king, <a href="/wiki/Kigeli_V_of_Rwanda" title="Kigeli V of Rwanda">Kigeli V</a>, to figurehead status;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199954_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199954-30">[30]</a></sup> Kigeli later fled the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESabar2013_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESabar2013-31">[31]</a></sup> Despite continued anti-Tutsi violence, Belgium organised local elections in mid-1960, with Hutu parties gaining control of almost all comunes, effectively ending the revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199952_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199952-32">[32]</a></sup> Logiest and Hutu leader <a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%A9goire_Kayibanda" title="Grégoire Kayibanda">Grégoire Kayibanda</a> declared the country an autonomous republic in 1961,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199953_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199953-33">[33]</a></sup> and it became independent in 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199954_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199954-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the revolution progressed, more than 336,000 Tutsi left Rwanda to escape the Hutu purges,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199962_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199962-34">[34]</a></sup> settling primarily in the four neighbouring countries of Burundi, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zaire" title="Zaire">Zaire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002160.E2.80.93161_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002160.E2.80.93161-35">[35]</a></sup> The Tutsi exiles, unlike the Banyarwanda who migrated during the pre-colonial and colonial era, were regarded as refugees in their host countries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199963.E2.80.9364_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199963.E2.80.9364-36">[36]</a></sup> and began almost immediately to agitate for a return to Rwanda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199955.E2.80.9356_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199955.E2.80.9356-37">[37]</a></sup> Some formed armed groups, known as <i>inyenzi</i> (cockroaches),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199954_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199954-30">[30]</a></sup> who from late 1960 launched attacks into Rwanda from the neighbouring countries, with mixed success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199955_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199955-38">[38]</a></sup> The <i>inyenzi</i> attacks were themselves a driving force in propelling further refugees across the borders, as the government often responded to them with further attacks on Tutsi still residing in Rwanda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199954_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199954-30">[30]</a></sup> The largest <i>inyenzi</i> attack was a surprise assault in late 1963, advancing to positions close to Kigali.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199956_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199956-39">[39]</a></sup> The invaders were poorly equipped and organised, however, and the government defeated them, following up with the slaughter of an estimated 10,000 Tutsi within Rwanda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199956_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199956-39">[39]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_community" title="International community">international community</a> did little in response to these killings, and the defeat was the final blow for the <i>inyenzi</i>, who posed no further threat to Rwanda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199957_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199957-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the 1963–64 massacre of Tutsi and defeat of the <i>inyenzi</i>, Kayibanda and PARMEHUTU ruled Rwanda unchecked for the next decade, overseeing a Hutu hegemony on power and influence, justified through the mantra of "demographic majority and democracy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199958_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199958-41">[41]</a></sup> The regime did not tolerate dissent, ruled in a top down manner similar to the pre-revolution feudal monarchy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199957_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199957-40">[40]</a></sup> and promoted a deeply catholic and virtuous ethos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199959_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199959-42">[42]</a></sup> By the early 1970s, this policy had made Rwanda very isolated from the rest of the world, and a rebellion began within the ranks of the Hutu elite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199960_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199960-43">[43]</a></sup> In 1973, <a href="/wiki/Juv%C3%A9nal_Habyarimana" title="Juvénal Habyarimana">Juvénal Habyarimana</a>, a senior army commander, organised <a href="/wiki/1973_Rwandan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1973 Rwandan coup d'état">a coup</a>, killing Kayibanda and assuming the presidency,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199974.E2.80.9376_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199974.E2.80.9376-44">[44]</a></sup> ruling Rwanda as a one-party state under his <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Movement_for_Democracy_and_Development" title="National Republican Movement for Democracy and Development">National Republican Movement for Democracy and Development</a> (MRND) party. Pro-Hutu discrimination continued under Habyarimana, but there was greater economic prosperity and a reduced amount of violence against Tutsi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199974.E2.80.9376_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199974.E2.80.9376-44">[44]</a></sup> In the late 1980s, there was a global coffee price collapse;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199984_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199984-45">[45]</a></sup> this meant a loss of revenue of many of Rwanda's wealthy elite, leading them to seek greater political power, to gain access to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foreign_aid" title="Foreign aid">foreign aid</a> receipts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199984_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199984-45">[45]</a></sup> The most powerful of these were the family of the first lady <a href="/wiki/Agathe_Habyarimana" title="Agathe Habyarimana">Agathe Habyarimana</a>, who were known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Akazu" title="Akazu">akazu</a></i> or <i>clan de Madame</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199985_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199985-46">[46]</a></sup> In April 1988, the <i>akazu</i> murdered <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Stanislas_Muyuya&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Stanislas Muyuya (page does not exist)">Stanislas Muyuya</a>, a close ally of the president, and possible successor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199984_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199984-45">[45]</a></sup> and Habyarimana became increasingly reliant on them for his rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199987_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199987-47">[47]</a></sup> The reduction in coffee income also forced the government into a large reduction in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_budget" title="National budget">national budget</a>, which increased the tension as the impoverished population struggled to survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199987_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199987-47">[47]</a></sup> In 1990, following the advice of <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Mitterrand" title="François Mitterrand">President Mitterrand</a> of France,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199989_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199989-48">[48]</a></sup> Habyarimana declared a commitment to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Multi-party_politics&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Multi-party politics (page does not exist)">multi-party politics</a>, but did not take any action to bring it about.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199990_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199990-49">[49]</a></sup> Student protests followed, and by late 1990 the country was in crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199990_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199990-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_of_the_RPF_and_preparation_for_war">Formation of the RPF and preparation for war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rwandan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Formation of the RPF and preparation for war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1979, Ugandan dictator <a href="/wiki/Idi_Amin" title="Idi Amin">Idi Amin</a> <a href="/wiki/Uganda%E2%80%93Tanzania_War" title="Uganda–Tanzania War">was defeated</a> by an alliance of the Tanzanian army and Ugandan rebels;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEState_House.2C_Republic_of_Uganda_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEState_House.2C_Republic_of_Uganda-50">[50]</a></sup> among the rebel fighters were <a href="/wiki/Fred_Rwigyema" title="Fred Rwigyema">Fred Rwigyema</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Kagame" title="Paul Kagame">Paul Kagame</a>, Rwandan Tutsi refugees who had joined <a href="/wiki/Yoweri_Museveni" title="Yoweri Museveni">Yoweri Museveni</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Front_for_National_Salvation" title="Front for National Salvation">Front for National Salvation</a> (FRONASA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199968_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199968-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Milton_Obote" title="Milton Obote">Milton Obote</a> assumed the Ugandan presidency, and began persecution and discrimination against the Tutsi refugees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199967_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199967-52">[52]</a></sup> In response, the refugees formed the Rwandan Refugees Welfare Association, which became the Rwandan Alliance for National Unity (RANU) the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199967_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199967-52">[52]</a></sup> Museveni was a cabinet member in the transition government, and Rwigyema, Kagame and some other Rwandan refugees remained allegiant to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200820_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200820-53">[53]</a></sup> Obote won the <a href="/wiki/Ugandan_general_election,_1980" title="Ugandan general election, 1980">1980 general election</a>, but Museveni disputed the result, and he, Rwigyema and Kagame withdrew from the new government in protest, forming the rebel <a href="/wiki/National_Resistance_Army" title="National Resistance Army">National Resistance Army</a> (NRA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssociated_Press_.28I.291981_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssociated_Press_.28I.291981-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200839_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200839-55">[55]</a></sup> The army's goal was to overthrow Obote's government, in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Ugandan_Bush_War" title="Ugandan Bush War">Ugandan Bush War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200839_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200839-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENganda2009_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENganda2009-56">[56]</a></sup> President Obote remained hostile to the Rwandan refugees; RANU was forced into exile in 1981, relocating to <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a>, Kenya,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199963_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199963-57">[57]</a></sup> and in 1982 <a href="/wiki/Ankole" title="Ankole">Ankole</a> youths attacked the Rwandans, with the authority of Obote,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199969_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199969-58">[58]</a></sup> causing many more to join Museveni's NRA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199970_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199970-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1986, the NRA captured Kampala with a force of 14,000 soldiers, including 500 Rwandans, and formed a new government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200847_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200847-60">[60]</a></sup> After Museveni was inaugurated as president, he appointed Kagame and Rwigyema as senior officers in the new Ugandan army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200850.E2.80.9351_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200850.E2.80.9351-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson_.28I.292000_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson_.28I.292000-62">[62]</a></sup> The experience of the Bush War gave Rwigyema and Kagame inspiration to consider an attack against Rwanda, and as well as fulfilling their army duties, they began building a covert network of Rwandan Tutsi refugees within the army's ranks, intended as the nucleus for such an attack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200851.E2.80.9352_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200851.E2.80.9352-63">[63]</a></sup> With the pro-refugee Museveni in power, RANU was able to move back to Kampala. At its 1987 convention, it renamed itself to the <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_Patriotic_Front" title="Rwandan Patriotic Front">Rwandan Patriotic Front</a> (RPF), and it too committed to returning the refugees to Rwanda by any means possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199973_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199973-64">[64]</a></sup> In 1988, a leadership crisis within the RPF prompted Fred Rwigyema to intervene in the organisation and take control, replacing <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Peter_Bayingana&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Peter Bayingana (page does not exist)">Peter Bayingana</a> as RPF president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBamurangirwa201380_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBamurangirwa201380-65">[65]</a></sup> Kagame and other senior members of Rwigyema's Rwandan entourage within the NRA also joined, with Kagame assuming the vice-presidency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBamurangirwa201380_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBamurangirwa201380-65">[65]</a></sup> Bayingana remained as the other vice-president, but resented the loss of the leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBamurangirwa201380_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBamurangirwa201380-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rwandan President Juvénal Habyarimana was aware of the increasing number of Tutsi exiles in the Ugandan army, and made representations to President Museveni on the matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200853_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200853-66">[66]</a></sup> At the same time, many native Ugandans began criticising Museveni over his appointment of Rwandan refugees to senior positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002175_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002175-67">[67]</a></sup> He therefore demoted Kagame and Rwigyema.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200853_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200853-66">[66]</a></sup> They remained de facto senior officers, but the change caused them to accelerate their plans to invade Rwanda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200853.E2.80.9354_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200853.E2.80.9354-68">[68]</a></sup> In 1990, a dispute in south western Uganda between Ugandan ranch owners and squatters on their land, many of whom were Rwandans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002176_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002176-69">[69]</a></sup> led to a wider debate on indigeneity and eventually to the explicit labelling of all Rwandan refugees as non-citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002182_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002182-70">[70]</a></sup> Realising the precariousness of their own positions, and the opportunity afforded by both the renewed drive of refugees to leave Uganda, and the precarious Rwandan domestic scene, Rwigyema and Kagame decided to effect their invasion plans immediately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200857_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200857-71">[71]</a></sup> It is likely that President Museveni knew of the RPF and its planned invasion, but did not explicitly support it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199997.E2.80.9398_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199997.E2.80.9398-72">[72]</a></sup> Museveni claimed ignorance, announcing years later that the RPF had launched the invasion "without prior consultation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002183_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002183-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Course_of_the_war">Course of the war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rwandan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Course of the war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1990_invasion_and_death_of_Rwigyema">1990 invasion and death of Rwigyema</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rwandan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1990 invasion and death of Rwigyema">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At 2:30 pm on 1 October 1990, fifty RPF rebels deserted their Ugandan army posts and crossed the border from Uganda into Rwanda, killing a customs guard at the Kagitumba border post.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199993_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199993-74">[74]</a></sup> They were followed by hundreds more rebels, dressed in the uniforms of the <a href="/wiki/Uganda_People%27s_Defence_Force" title="Uganda People's Defence Force">Ugandan national army</a> and carrying stolen Ugandan weaponry, including <a href="/wiki/Machine_gun" title="Machine gun">machine guns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Autocannon" title="Autocannon">autocannons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(weapon)" title="Mortar (weapon)">mortars</a>, and Soviet <a href="/wiki/BM-21_Grad" title="BM-21 Grad">BM-21 multiple rocket launchers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199993_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199993-74">[74]</a></sup> Around 2,500 of the Ugandan army's 4,000 Rwandan soldiers took part in the invasion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199993_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199993-74">[74]</a></sup> accompanied by 800 civilians, including medical staff and messengers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200865_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200865-75">[75]</a></sup> Both President Yoweri Museveni of Uganda and President Habyarimana of Rwanda were in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> attending the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Summit_for_Children" title="World Summit for Children">World Summit for Children</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiles1990_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiles1990-76">[76]</a></sup> In the first few days of fighting, the RPF made significant progress, advancing 60 km (37 mi) south to the town of Gabiro.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199994_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199994-77">[77]</a></sup> Their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rwandan_Armed_Forces" title="Rwandan Armed Forces">Rwandan Armed Forces</a> (FAR) opponents, fighting for Habyarimana's government, were numerically superior, with 5,200 soldiers, and possessed armoured cars and helicopters supplied by France, but the RPF benefitted from the element of surprise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199994_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199994-77">[77]</a></sup> The Ugandan government set up road blocks across the west of Uganda, to prevent further desertions and to block the rebels from returning to Uganda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199994_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199994-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 2 October, the RPF suffered a significant reversal, when its leader, Fred Rwigyema was shot in the head and killed. There is a dispute about the exact circumstances of Rwigyema's death; the official line of Kagame's government,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGovernment_of_Rwanda2009_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGovernment_of_Rwanda2009-78">[78]</a></sup> and the version mentioned by historian <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9rard_Prunier" title="Gérard Prunier">Gérard Prunier</a> in his 1995 book on the subject, was that Rwigyema was killed by a stray bullet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199995.E2.80.9396_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199995.E2.80.9396-79">[79]</a></sup> In his 2009 book <i>Africa's World War</i>, however, Prunier states that it is likely Rwigyema was killed by his subcommander Peter Bayingana, following an argument over tactics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier200913.E2.80.9314_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier200913.E2.80.9314-80">[80]</a></sup> According to this account, Rwigyema was conscious of the need to move slowly, and attempt to win over the Hutu in Rwanda before assaulting Kigali, whereas Bayingana and fellow subcommander <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Chris_Bunyenyezi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Chris Bunyenyezi (page does not exist)">Chris Bunyenyezi</a>, wished to strike hard and fast, to achieve power as soon as possible;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier200913.E2.80.9314_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier200913.E2.80.9314-80">[80]</a></sup> the argument boiled over, causing Bayingana to shoot Rwigyema dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier200913.E2.80.9314_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier200913.E2.80.9314-80">[80]</a></sup> Another senior RPF officer, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Stephen_Nduguta&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Stephen Nduguta (page does not exist)">Stephen Nduguta</a>, witnessed this shooting and informed President Museveni; Museveni sent his brother <a href="/wiki/Salim_Saleh" title="Salim Saleh">Salim Saleh</a> to investigate, and Saleh ordered Bayingana and Bunyenyezi's arrest and eventual execution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier200914_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier200914-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>When news of the RPF offensive broke, France and Belgium sent troops to Kigali to assist the Rwandan military in fighting the invasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999101_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999101-82">[82]</a></sup> The Belgian presence was short lived, because its laws prevented the army from intervening in a civil war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvern200014_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvern200014-83">[83]</a></sup> France, in contrast, supported the regime and gave significant military and financial support. In a military operation code-named <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Op%C3%A9ration_Noro%C3%AEt&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Opération Noroît (page does not exist)">Noroît</a>, France deployed 125 soldiers, who had been based in the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a>, to support the FAR.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hikabisa_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-hikabisa-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voltaire_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-voltaire-85">[85]</a></sup> France insisted that its forces had been deployed strictly to protect its nationals, but the parachute companies set up positions blocking the RPF advance to the capital and <a href="/wiki/Kigali_International_Airport" title="Kigali International Airport">Kigali International Airport</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvern200014_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvern200014-83">[83]</a></sup> Zairian President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mobutu" title="Mobutu">Mobutu</a> also assisted Habyarimana, sending several hundred troops of the elite <a href="/wiki/Special_Presidential_Division" title="Special Presidential Division">Special Presidential Division</a> (DSP)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999101_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999101-82">[82]</a></sup> Unlike the French, the Zairian troops went straight to the front line and began fighting the RPF rather than occupying defensive positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999101_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999101-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the night of 4 October, the Rwandan government staged a fake attack on Kigali with gunfire and explosions around the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999102_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999102-86">[86]</a></sup> The French were deceived, believing that the RPF were responsible for the attack, and immediately increased their troop numbers to 600.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999102_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999102-86">[86]</a></sup> The government followed the fake attack with anti-Tutsi rhetoric, enouraging Hutu citizens to arrest Tutsi suspected of supporting the RPF.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvern200014_15_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvern200014_15-87">[87]</a></sup> With French and Zairian assistance, and benefiting from the loss of RPF morale after Rwigyema's death, the FAR enjoyed a major advantage; gradually they regained all the ground the RPF had taken. The rebels were eventually pushed back to the Ugandan border on 30 October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199996_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199996-88">[88]</a></sup> Many soldiers deserted, some crossed back into Uganda, while others went into hiding in the <a href="/wiki/Akagera_National_Park" title="Akagera National Park">Akagera National Park</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199996_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199996-88">[88]</a></sup> The Rwandan government announced that they had won the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier199996_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier199996-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kagame.27s_reorganization_of_the_RPF">Kagame's reorganization of the RPF</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rwandan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Kagame's reorganization of the RPF">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RwandaVolcanoAndLake_cropped2.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of a lake with one of the Virunga Mountains behind, partially in cloud" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/RwandaVolcanoAndLake_cropped2.jpg/220px-RwandaVolcanoAndLake_cropped2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/RwandaVolcanoAndLake_cropped2.jpg/330px-RwandaVolcanoAndLake_cropped2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/RwandaVolcanoAndLake_cropped2.jpg/440px-RwandaVolcanoAndLake_cropped2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RwandaVolcanoAndLake_cropped2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Virunga_Mountains" title="Virunga Mountains">Virunga Mountains</a>, the RPF base from 1990 to 1991.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Paul Kagame was still in the United States at the time of the October invasion, but had already informed the commanders at Fort Leavenworth of his intention to leave the course and return to Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200864_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200864-89">[89]</a></sup> When Kagame learned of Rwigyema's death, he departed immediately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200867_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200867-90">[90]</a></sup> He flew through <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> and <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a>, eventually arriving at <a href="/wiki/Entebbe_International_Airport" title="Entebbe International Airport">Entebbe Airport</a> where he was given safe passage by a friend in the Ugandan secret service;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200875_76_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200875_76-91">[91]</a></sup> the police considered arresting him, but with Museveni out of the country, and no specific orders, they allowed him to pass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200876_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200876-92">[92]</a></sup> Kagame was then driven to the border and crossed into Rwanda early on 15 October, to take command of the RPF troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200876_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200876-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>After spending a few days with the senior officers gathering intelligence, Kagame decided his soldiers were too demoralised to continue fighting, and withdrew most of the army from north eastern Rwanda, moving them to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virunga_mountains" title="Virunga mountains">Virunga mountains</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200879_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200879-93">[93]</a></sup> Some soldiers remained behind as a decoy, carrying out small scale attacks on Rwandan forces, which meant the Rwandan army did not realise that the majority of forces had departed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200880_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200880-94">[94]</a></sup> The rugged high altitude terrain of the Virungas offered considerable protection from attacks if the RPF were to be discovered there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999114.E2.80.93115_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999114.E2.80.93115-95">[95]</a></sup> The trek west took almost a week, and the soldiers crossed the border into Uganda several times, with the permission of President Museveni, taking advantage of personal friendships between the RPF soldiers and their ex-colleagues in the Ugandan army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200880_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200880-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>Conditions in the Virungas were very harsh for the RPF. At an altitude of 5,000 metres (16,000 ft),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999115_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999115-96">[96]</a></sup> there was no easy supply of food or supplies and, lacking warm clothing, several soldiers froze to death in the altitude-induced cold temperatures led to the death or loss of limbs of several soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200880_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200880-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999115_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999115-96">[96]</a></sup> Kagame spent the next two months reorganising the army, without carrying out any military operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999115_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999115-96">[96]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alexis_Kanyarengwe&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Alexis Kanyarengwe (page does not exist)">Alexis Kanyarengwe</a>, a Hutu <a href="/wiki/Colonel" title="Colonel">Colonel</a> who had previously worked with Habyarimana but had fallen out with him and gone into exile, joined the RPF and was appointed chairman of the organisation;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999115_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999115-96">[96]</a></sup> the appointment of Kanyarengwe was motivated by a desire to appear inclusive, although most of the other senior recruits at the time were Ugandan based Tutsi similar to Kagame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999115_96-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999115-96">[96]</a></sup> Rank and file numbers grew steadily, with volunteers coming from the exile communities in Burundi, Zaire and other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999116_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999116-97">[97]</a></sup> Kagame maintained tight discipline in his army, with a regimented training routine, and a large set of rules for soldiers' conduct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200883_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200883-98">[98]</a></sup> Soldiers were expected to pay for goods they purchased in the community, not indulge in alcohol or drugs, and uphold standards to establish a good reputation for the RPF amongst the local population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200882_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200882-99">[99]</a></sup> Certain offences, such as murder, rape, and desertion, were punishable by death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200883_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200883-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>The RPF carried out a big fund raising programme, spearheaded by financial commissioner <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aloisia_Inyumba&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aloisia Inyumba (page does not exist)">Aloisia Inyumba</a> from an office in Kampala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999115_96-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999115-96">[96]</a></sup> They received donations from Tutsi exiles around the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200882_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200882-99">[99]</a></sup> including North America, Europe and Africa, as well as from some businessmen within Rwanda who had fallen out with the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999117_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999117-100">[100]</a></sup> The sums involved were not enormous, but with tight financial discipline and a leadership willing to lead frugal lives, the Front was able to grow its operational capability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999118_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999118-101">[101]</a></sup> The RPF sourced its weapons and ammunition from a variety of sources, including the open market, taking advantage of an excess of redundant weaponry at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999118_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999118-101">[101]</a></sup> It is likely they also received weaponry from officers in the Ugandan army; according to Gérard Prunier, Ugandans who had fought with Kagame in the Bush War remained loyal to him and passed weaponry in a clandestine manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999119_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999119-102">[102]</a></sup> Museveni likely knew of this, but was able to claim ignorance when dealing with the international community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999119_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999119-102">[102]</a></sup> Museveni later said that "faced with [a] <i>fait accompli</i> situation by our Rwandan brothers," Uganda went "to help the RPF, materially, so that they are not defeated because that would have been detrimental to the Tutsi people of Rwanda and would not have been good for Uganda's stability."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002183_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMamdani2002183-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attack_on_Ruhengeri.2C_January_1991">Attack on Ruhengeri, January 1991</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rwandan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Attack on Ruhengeri, January 1991">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ruhengeri.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1500" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ruhengeri.jpg/225px-Ruhengeri.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ruhengeri.jpg/338px-Ruhengeri.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ruhengeri.jpg/450px-Ruhengeri.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ruhengeri.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The town of <a href="/wiki/Ruhengeri" title="Ruhengeri">Ruhengeri</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Virunga_Mountains" title="Virunga Mountains">Virunga Mountains</a> in the background</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After three months of inactivity and regrouping, Kagame decided in January 1991 that the RPF was ready to begin fighting again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200887_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200887-103">[103]</a></sup> The target chosen for the attack was the northern city of <a href="/wiki/Ruhengeri" title="Ruhengeri">Ruhengeri</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200887_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200887-103">[103]</a></sup> which lies immediately south of the Virunga mountain chain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEITMB_Publishing_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEITMB_Publishing-104">[104]</a></sup> The city was the best choice from a practical point of view, being the only provincial capital that could be attacked quickly from the Virungas, maintaining an element of surprise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999119_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999119-102">[102]</a></sup> Kagame also favoured an attack on Ruhengeri for cultural reasons. President Habyarimana, as well as his wife and her powerful family, came from the north west of Rwanda and most Rwandans regarded the region was as the heartland of the regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999119_102-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999119-102">[102]</a></sup> An attack there guaranteed that the population would become aware of the RPF's presence, and Kagame hoped the attack would destabilise the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200888_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200888-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the night of 22 January, seven hundred RPF fighters moved down from the mountains and waited in hidden locations around the city; they were assisted by RPF sympathisers residing in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200888_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200888-105">[105]</a></sup> On the morning of the 23rd they attacked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999120_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999120-106">[106]</a></sup> The Rwandan forces in the area were taken by surprise and were mostly unable to defend against the invasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200888_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200888-105">[105]</a></sup> The Rwandan police and army did succeed for a while in repelling the invasion in some areas, killing a number of rebel fighters in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200888_105-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200888-105">[105]</a></sup> It is likely the government's FAR forces were assisted by French troops, as the French government later rewarded around fifteen French paratroopers for having taken part in the rearguard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200888_105-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200888-105">[105]</a></sup> By noon, however, the defending forces were defeated, and the RPF held the whole of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200889_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200889-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the principal RPF targets in Ruhengeri was the prison, which was Rwanda's largest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999119_102-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999119-102">[102]</a></sup> When he learned of the invasion the warden, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_Uwihoreye&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Charles Uwihoreye (page does not exist)">Charles Uwihoreye</a>, telephoned the government in Kigali, to request instructions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999120_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999120-106">[106]</a></sup> He spoke to Colonel <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Elie_Sagatwa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Elie Sagatwa (page does not exist)">Elie Sagatwa</a>, one of the <i>akazu</i>, who ordered him to kill every inmate in the prison, to avoid escape and defections during the fighting,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200888_105-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200888-105">[105]</a></sup> and to prevent high-profile political prisoners and former insiders from sharing secret information with the RPF.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999119_102-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999119-102">[102]</a></sup> Uwihoreye refused to obey, even after Sagatwa called him to repeat the order, having confirmed it with the president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200888_105-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200888-105">[105]</a></sup> Eventually, the RPF stormed the buildings, and the prisoners were saved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200889_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200889-107">[107]</a></sup> Several prisoners were recruited into the RPF, including <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Theoneste_Lizinde&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Theoneste Lizinde (page does not exist)">Theoneste Lizinde</a>, a former close ally of President Habyarimana, who had been arrested following a failed coup attempt in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200889_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200889-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999119_102-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999119-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>The RPF forces held Ruhengeri through the afternoon of 23 January, before withdrawing back into the mountains for the night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200889_107-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200889-107">[107]</a></sup> The Rwandan government sent troops to the city the following day, and a <a href="/wiki/State_of_emergency" title="State of emergency">state of emergency</a> was declared, with strict curfews in Ruhengeri and the surrounding area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200889_107-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200889-107">[107]</a></sup> The RPF raided the city every night, fighting with the Rwandan army forces, and the country was back at war for the first time since the October invasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200889_107-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200889-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Guerrilla_war.2C_1991.E2.80.9392">Guerrilla war, 1991–92</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rwandan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Guerrilla war, 1991–92">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the action in Ruhengeri, the RPF began to carry out a classic hit-and-run style guerrilla war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999135_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999135-108">[108]</a></sup> The Rwandan army massed troops across the north of the country, occupying key positions and shelling RPF hideouts in the Virunga mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200890_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200890-109">[109]</a></sup> However, the mountainous terrain prevented them from launching an all out assault.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200890_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200890-109">[109]</a></sup> Paul Kagame's troops also attacked the FAR forces repeatedly and frequently, keen to ensure that the diplomatic and psychological effect of the RPF's resurgence was not lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200891_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200891-110">[110]</a></sup> Kagame employed tactics such as attacking simultaneously in up to ten different locations across the north of the country, to prevent his opponents concentrating their force in any one place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200891_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200891-110">[110]</a></sup> This low intensity war continued for many months, both sides launching successful attacks on the other, and neither able to gain the upper hand in the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200891_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200891-110">[110]</a></sup> The RPF made some territorial gains; this included capturing the border town of <a href="/wiki/Gatuna" title="Gatuna">Gatuna</a>, blocking Rwanda's access to the port of <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Corridor" title="Northern Corridor">Northern Corridor</a>, and forcing all trade to go through the longer and costlier <a href="/wiki/Central_Corridor_(Africa)" title="Central Corridor (Africa)">Central Corridor</a> route through Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999135_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999135-108">[108]</a></sup> By late 1991, the RPF controlled 5% of Rwanda, setting up its new headquarters in an abandoned tea factory close to <a href="/wiki/Mulindi" title="Mulindi">Mulindi</a>, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byumba_province" title="Byumba province">Byumba province</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200896_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200896-111">[111]</a></sup> Many Hutu civilians residing in areas captured by the RPF fled back to government held areas, creating a large population of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internally_displaced_persons" title="Internally displaced persons">internally displaced persons</a> in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999136_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999136-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>The renewed warfare had two effects on the domestic situation in Rwanda itself. The first was a resurgence of violence against Tutsi still residing in the country. Beginning with the slaughter of 30–60 <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bagogwe&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bagogwe (page does not exist)">Bagogwe</a> Tutsi pastoralists near <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kinigi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kinigi (page does not exist)">Kinigi</a>, and then spreading south and west to Ruhengeri and <a href="/wiki/Gisenyi" title="Gisenyi">Gisenyi</a>, Hutu activists killed up to 1,000 Tutsi in attacks authorised by local officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999136.E2.80.93137_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999136.E2.80.93137-113">[113]</a></sup> These attacks continued until June 1991, when a deal was reached to allow potential victims to move to safer areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999136.E2.80.93137_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999136.E2.80.93137-113">[113]</a></sup> The <i>akazu</i> also began a major propaganda campaign, broadcasting and publishing material designed to persuade the Hutu population that the Tutsi were a completely separate and alien people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200897_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200897-114">[114]</a></sup> This included the <a href="/wiki/Hutu_Ten_Commandments" title="Hutu Ten Commandments">Hutu Ten Commandments</a>, a set of "rules" published in <i>Kangura</i> magazine mandating Hutu supremacy in all aspects of Rwandan life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200897_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200897-114">[114]</a></sup> In response, the RPF started broadcasting from Uganda into Rwanda on its own propaganda radio station, called <a href="/wiki/Radio_Muhabura" title="Radio Muhabura">Radio Muhabura</a>; this was never hugely popular, but gained listenership through 1992 and 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>The second development was that President Habyarimana announced that he was implementing multi-party politics into the country, under intense pressure from the international community, including his most loyal ally <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200897_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200897-114">[114]</a></sup> Habyarimana had originally announced their introduction in mid-1990, and a number of opposition groups had formed in the months since, including the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Democratic_Movement" title="Republican Democratic Movement">Republican Democratic Movement</a> (MDR), <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_(Rwanda)" title="Social Democratic Party (Rwanda)">Social Democratic Party</a> (PSD) and the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(Rwanda)" title="Liberal Party (Rwanda)">Liberal Party</a> (PL),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999123.E2.80.93124_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999123.E2.80.93124-116">[116]</a></sup> but the one-party state law had remained in place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999127_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999127-117">[117]</a></sup> In mid-1991, Habyarimana officially allowed multi-party politics to begin, a change which saw a plethora of additional new parties come into existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999127_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999127-117">[117]</a></sup> Many of these had manifestos which favoured full democracy and rapprochement with the RPF,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200897_114-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200897-114">[114]</a></sup> but in reality were quite innefective and had no actual political influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999127_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999127-117">[117]</a></sup> The older opposition groups registered themselves as official parties, and the country was notionally moving towards a multi-party inclusive cabinet with proper representation, but progress was continually hampered by the regime. The last opposition party to form was the <a href="/wiki/Coalition_for_the_Defence_of_the_Republic" title="Coalition for the Defence of the Republic">Coalition for the Defence of the Republic</a> (CDR), which was more hardline Hutu than Habyarimana's own party and had close links to the <i>akazu</i>, the family of the first lady.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200898_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200898-118">[118]</a></sup> Progress towards multi-partyism was slow. A cabinet set up in October 1991 contained almost no opposition at all, and the administrative hierarchy across the country recognised the authority only of Habyarimana's MRND party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999134_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999134-119">[119]</a></sup> The regime frequently used violence as a tool to hamper reform, justifying its actions as anti-RPF security measures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999134_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999134-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another one-party cabinet was announced in January 1992, which prompted large scale protests in Kigali, and finally forced Habyarimana to make real concessions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer2008103_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer2008103-120">[120]</a></sup> He promised for the first time to negotiate with the RPF;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer2008103_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer2008103-120">[120]</a></sup> a multiparty cabinet formed in April, still dominated by the MRND, but with opposition figures in some key positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999145_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999145-121">[121]</a></sup> The opposition members of this cabinet met with the RPF, and were successful in negotiating a ceasefire. In July 1992 the rebels agreed to stop fighting, and the parties began a peace negotiation process headquartered in the Tanzanian city of <a href="/wiki/Arusha" title="Arusha">Arusha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999150_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999150-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peace_process.2C_1992.E2.80.9393">Peace process, 1992–93</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rwandan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Peace process, 1992–93">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The peace process was a complex one, with four distinct groups involved, each with its own agenda. The Hutu hardliners, centred around the family of Agathe Habyarimana, were represented by the CDR, as well as extremists within the president's own MRND party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999161_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999161-123">[123]</a></sup> This group opposed the entire peace process, being unwilling to cede any ground to the RPF or the Tutsi, whom they continued to brand as enemies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999154_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999154-124">[124]</a></sup> The official opposition, outside of the CDR, were generally had much more democratic and conciliatory aims, but they also remained deeply suspicious of the RPF, whom they saw as trying to upset the "democratic" Hutu leadership begun in the 1959 revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999151_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999151-125">[125]</a></sup> Paul Kagame engaged with the peace process, against the advice of some of his senior officers, and in the knowledge that many on the otherside were hardliners, because he feared that shunning it would weaken the RPF politically and lose them considerable goodwill internationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer200897_114-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer200897-114">[114]</a></sup> Finally, President Habyarimana himself sought primarily to hold on to his power in whatever form he could, which meant publicly striving for a middle ground compromise solution, but privately obstructing the process and trying to delay change to the status quo for as long as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999161_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999161-123">[123]</a></sup> Habyarimana recognised the danger posed to him by the radical Hutu faction, and attempted in mid-1992 to remove them from senior army positions; this effort was only partially successful; <i>akazu</i> affiliates <a href="/wiki/Augustin_Ndindiliyimana" title="Augustin Ndindiliyimana">Augustin Ndindiliyimana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9oneste_Bagosora" title="Théoneste Bagosora">Théoneste Bagosora</a> remained in powerful posts, providing them with a link to power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999167_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999167-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>The delegates at the negotiations in Arusha made some progress through the latter half of 1992, despite wrangling at home between Habyarimana and the hardliners that compromised the government officials' negotiating power in the talks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999162_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999162-127">[127]</a></sup> In August, the parties agreed a "pluralistic transitional government" which would include the RPF.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999162_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999162-127">[127]</a></sup> The CDR and hardline faction of the MRND reacted violently to this; feeling sidelined by the developing Arusha process,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999163_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999163-128">[128]</a></sup> they began killing Tutsi civilians in the <a href="/wiki/Kibuye,_Rwanda" title="Kibuye, Rwanda">Kibuye</a> area; 85 were killed in total, with 500 homes burned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999162_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999162-127">[127]</a></sup> Historian Gérard Prunier names late 1992 as the time when the idea of a genocidal "final solution" to kill every Tutsi in Rwanda was first mooted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999169_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999169-129">[129]</a></sup> Hardliners were busy setting up parallel institutions within the official organs of state, including the army, from which they hoped to effect a move away from the more conciliatory tone adopted by Habyarimana and the moderate opposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999169_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999169-129">[129]</a></sup> Their goal was to supplant the official government as the perceived source of power in the country amongst the Hutu masses, to maintain the line that the RPF and Tutsi more generally were a threat to Hutu freedoms, and to find a way to thwart any agreement negotiated in Arusha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999170_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999170-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>The situation deteriorated in early 1993, when the teams in Arusha signed a full power sharing agreement, dividing government positions between the MRND, RPF and the other opposition parties but not the CDR;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999173_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999173-131">[131]</a></sup> this government was supposed to rule the country with a transitional government until the country was peaceful enough for free and fair elections to be held.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999173_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999173-131">[131]</a></sup> The agreement was reasonably equitable given the balance of power at the time, and Habyarimana, the mainstream opposition and the RPF accepted it, but the CDR and hardline MRND officers were violently opposed;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999173_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999173-131">[131]</a></sup> MRND national secretary <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mathieu_Ngirumpatse&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mathieu Ngirumpatse (page does not exist)">Mathieu Ngirumpatse</a> announced that the party would not respect the agreement, contradicting both the president and the party's negotiators in Arusha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999173_131-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999173-131">[131]</a></sup> The hardliners organised demonstrations across the country, and mobilised their supporters within the army and populace to begin a much larger scale killing spree than those that had previously occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999174_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999174-132">[132]</a></sup> The violence engulfed the whole north west of Rwanda and lasted for six days, with numerous houses burned and hundreds of Tutsi killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999174_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999174-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer2008104_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer2008104-133">[133]</a></sup> Paul Kagame reacted to the escalating violence, reacting to stories brought to him first hand by survivors seeking refuge in rebel territory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer2008104_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer2008104-133">[133]</a></sup> by temporarily pulling out of the Arusha process and declaring an resumption of war, ending the six month cease-fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999174_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrunier1999174-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="RPF_offensive.2C_February_1993">RPF offensive, February 1993</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rwandan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: RPF offensive, February 1993">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RwandaTerritoryAfterFeb1993.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2312" data-file-width="2632" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/RwandaTerritoryAfterFeb1993.png/220px-RwandaTerritoryAfterFeb1993.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/RwandaTerritoryAfterFeb1993.png/330px-RwandaTerritoryAfterFeb1993.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/RwandaTerritoryAfterFeb1993.png/440px-RwandaTerritoryAfterFeb1993.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RwandaTerritoryAfterFeb1993.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
